0307474259|4.0|With crystalline clarity, science writer - Gary Taubes breaks down the process on how people accumulate excess fat and why under conventional and fad dieting it is so hard to lose pounds over the long haul. Taubes then spends the next 220+ pages offering a very lucid, layman's explanation on what the consumption of said elements does to one's body and how it ultimately creates a vicious cycle of insulin resistance and other inconveniences that ultimately leads to the weight gain that has been a curse to modern society.<br /><br />Keep in mind that this is not a diet book that offers a detailed regimen and recipes to put one on a fast track to lean and trim.  Taubes solution is offered within the first few pages and the remainder of the book is a detailed explanation to support his arguments.

B0031RS64G|4.0|[[ASIN:0765322889 Gardens of the Moon (Malazan Book of the Fallen)]] is the first book in a ten book series called The Malazan Book of the Fallen. The series is arguably the most epic in scope, the most complex in narrative style, and the most detailed in terms of cultural, sociological and religious aspects. Comparisons to other great fantasy epics will no doubt include [[ASIN:0618640150 The Lord of the Rings: 50th Anniversary, One Vol. Edition]] or [[ASIN:0553386794 A Game of Thrones (A Song of Ice and Fire, Book 1)]] or even [[ASIN:0812538366 The Wheel of Time, Boxed Set I, Books 1-3: The Eye of the World, The Great Hunt, The Dragon Reborn]]...but a better and more accurate comparison may be to Frank Herbert's magnificent sci-fi masterpiece, the [[ASIN:B001F0WXY0 Dune (40th Anniversary Edition) (Dune Chronicles, Book 1)]] chronicles, and Glen Cook's gritty and character-centric fantasy series [[ASIN:0812521390 The Black Company (Chronicles of The Black Company #1)]].<br /><br />[[ASIN:0765322889 Gardens of the Moon (Malazan Book of the Fallen)]] starts off much like [[ASIN:B001F0WXY0 Dune (40th Anniversary Edition) (Dune Chronicles, Book 1)]] and [[ASIN:0812521390 The Black Company (Chronicles of The Black Company #1)]], in that you are literally thrown into the middle of this fully realized world, replete with an ancient but still living prehistory (in the form of the T'lan Imass and the Jaghut), a powerful but aloof alien species (in the form of the Tiste Andii), and a host of elder gods who are anything but passive and who routinely interfere and direct the lives of the general populace.<br /><br />I remember when i was a teenager and I had heard of a movie called Dune which was apparently based upon a best-selling novel. The premise intrigued me to the point where I read the book and then immediately saw the movie by David Lynch. I did  not see the movie alone. I took my cousin with me. My female cousin who knew nothing of sci-fi or fantasy and who probably was looking for a movie like Gremlins or Ghostbusters, rather than a strange sci-fantasy like Dune. And the movie was incredibly strange. If i had not read the book...i don't think even I would have been able to pick up on all of the subtle nuances and grand scope of things without having read the book first. I can't imagine what it must have been like for my poor cousin, who was so confused and befuddled afterwards that all she could do was ask \\"What is the 'water of life'? and why did he keep saying 'the sleeper has awakened'?\\"<br /><br />I recount this story only to illustrate a potential problem point: readers who are looking for the standard/typical mode of fantasy storytelling which have become so predictable that many times we're not looking for diversity in narrative, but rather looking at what type of new \\"power\\" is being contested or won. No, Gardens of the Moon will be as baffling and mysterious and potentially frustrating for many readers who are looking for something straightforward and simple to explain. Such is not the case with this series. Steven Erikson has created a complex and living breathing world and populated with various races and cultures and countries and thrown them into this story.<br /><br />And what is the story? It's hard to say really. There are so many narrative plot points. But I think the fundamental story is that something happened thousands of years before, something so terrible and cataclysmic that the repercussions of the event have reverberated throughout history, affecting the elder races and which have now culminated into a series of devastating and world-ending consequences that the modern races and their current use of magic must now contend with the here and now.<br /><br />I having been reading this series for the past few years now, and am on the last few novels. These books are not easy to read, but they yield so much pleasure in terms of epic storytelling and the range of characters.<br /><br />I will point out two things that you may need to know about the entire series:<br />First, Steven Erikson believes that all characters, big and small, matter. You might agree with that viewpoint, but if you think about what that really means, it means that each and every little character gets their share of the page, meaning what would normally be a 400 page book could turn into a 1,000 page book because the lives and dreams of each and every character is detailed and told. hence the massive length of most of Erikson's books.<br />Second, Steven Erikson's prose style is almost as complex and flowery as his story and settings. This prose is as purple as purple can get. He tends to wax poetic and philosophically on things and may even meander here and there, but the beauty of the series is in the details. And my god, there are some details. Details that you may think aren't much when you first encounter them, but then you realize how they contribute to the overall understanding and appreciation of the entire story.<br /><br />But with that said, these books are marvelous. And if you can get through the first book, as dense and complex as it is, then you will be rewarded by the 2nd and 3rd books, [[ASIN:0765348799 Deadhouse Gates (The Malazan Book of the Fallen, Book 2)]] and Memories of Ice (The Malazan Book of the Fallen, Book 3), which are about as good as anything i've ever read. The book Deadhouse Gates (The Malazan Book of the Fallen, Book 2) will break your heart with its grueling story about Coltaine's Chain of Dogs. And Memories of Ice will astound you in its scope and power; it is perhaps Erikson's one Masterpiece, though some may argue that the entire series is what constitutes his masterwork. but if one were to give the word masterpiece to any one of his novels, it would probably have to be Memories of Ice.<br /><br />But in order to get there, you have to go through Gardens of the Moon (Malazan Book of the Fallen), which is not his best work, but the beginning of his greatest work.ings that you may need to know about the entire series:<br />First, Steven Erikson believes that all characters, big and small, matter. 0385040253|4.0|The Ashley Book of Knots is THE standard that other books are measured against.  It has quite a few positives.  It is perhaps one of the largest collection of knots every assembled, gives history and usage for most of the knots, and is accompanied by quite a few entertaining sea stories.  If you enjoy working with knots this book is a must have for your knotting library.  Now you may be wondering why I only gave this book 4 stars....<br /><br />The biggest con of this book has to deal with it's occasional lack of clarity for some of the more complex knot work.  As I am a novice at knot tying when I sometimes have difficulty tying these knots as the directions/pictures are not always clear.  For example; when tying some of the flat knots it shows how to start and end the knot but does not give clear directions for how to extend the knot beyond the diagram.  While this may not present a problem for those with experience it does present a problem for individuals like myself.<br /><br />Overall this book is well worth the expense and should be added to your library if you are interested in knots, their usage and history.

But I am a confirmed convert to e-book format, and have been thinning my collection of hard copies ... much prefer to carry around hundreds of my favorite books with me everywhere, for the weight of a single paperback!  :-)<br /><br />I was reminded of LISEY'S STORY recently, when a few pages of the Kindle edition appeared at the end of something else I was reading (possibly CELL, also by King).  With a sense of happy nostalgia, I started to read those pages ... and was, in a word, horrified by the terrible editing.  Words missing ... words misspelled ... incorrect words substituted (as if someone had done an automatic spellcheck and simply accepted every suggested change without bothering to see if they were appropriate).  I was hoping to come to the LISEY'S STORY page on Amazon and read reviews of the editing in the final Kindle edition, should I decide to buy it ... had the problems I noted in the sample been fixed?<br /><br />Instead, I find a stream of unhappy recent reviews of this book from readers who seem to have been genuinely bored or disappointed by it.  I know you can't please all the people all the time, but I'm honestly surprised by such negative reactions. I am a professional proofreader, and the sort of problems I'm complaining about should have been (at least mostly) picked up in a single proofing round of the prepared edition.  We're not talking about expensive weeks or months of editing here!  I know what proofreaders make, and it's ridiculous to suggest that taking this step would be somehow too expensive to do for a Kindle edition. B007SZ0E1K|5.0|Wonderful CPU!! I have 3 of these and one i7 3770K. The i5 3570K in my experience of the two has been a more stable chip for me.I can tell you, this chip is great!!! ITs stable, no matter what the settings. The integrated graphics are truly amazing considering they are coming from the CPU itself. You almost don't need a discreet graphics card (almost).  ; )<br /><br />One thing I did run into is one of the three chips I have of this variety could not handle RAM any higher than 2400 mhz. It would not run G Skill Trident-X 2666mhz RAM even on the XMP settings. No matter what I tried or what voltages etc. would not post anything higher than 2400 mhz. After conferring with some industry \\"in the knows\\" a conclusion has been drawn that the majority of the chips (CPU's) manufactured in Cost Rica(the 3rd Gen. i5 flavor) do not handle RAM higher than 2400 mhz. Apparently the IMC's (Internal Memory Controllers) on these chips just aren't robust enough to take the higher speed RAM's. At first I was worried that any of the 3570K chips would not run higher RAM speeds. Luckily some do. I was concerned that I might have to go with the 3770K's exclusively if I wanted faster ram speed settings. Glad this did not turn out to be the case.  The 3770k is at least $100 more expensive and I found this chip to be more to my liking. (more stable for me )<br /><br />One of the experts I conferred with indicated that out of 16 consumer retail CPU's (i5  3570K) chips, only 5 or 6 of the lot of 16 would run or post at any RAM setting higher than 2400mhz. Even though the board is rated for RAM speed up through 2800  (even higher settings into the 30k's is listed in this boards BIOS)<br /><br />That said I ordered 2 additional CPU's and luckily they both were manufactured in Malaysia  (marked \\"Malay\\")and both ran 2666 mhz RAM no problems. So I would have to agree there is some validity to what this gentleman was saying.<br /><br />So if your into overclocking and/or going to run RAM any faster than 2400 mhz I would make sure you go for the \\"Malaysia\\" manufactured chips instead of the \\"Costa Rica\\" chips. Its not guaranteed either way, as he did have some run at 2666 mhz from \\"Costa\\" however, chances are more that  you wont be able to run higher RAM speeds than the chances that you will. So far I am two for two on the Malaysian chips and one for one on the costa Rica chips. Either way, great CPU's and if you have no plans to use higher settings then by all means the Costa Rica chips are fine. Enjoy!!

B005MYEPXC|5.0|If you know Woody Allen's stand-up work from the 60s you'll know the European vacation sketch with Hemmingway and Gertrude Stein. This takes that singular idea and weaves it into a feature film - with all the strengths and weaknesses that implies.<br /><br />On the one hand it's funny and engaging, a treat of a film. It's well cast with Owen Wilson doing a good `Woody' turn; Marion Cotllard perfect as the coquettish muse and Corey Stoll stealing the show as a laugh-out-loud Hemmingway. Michael Sheen is also hysterical. The humour is a mix of contemporary digs, the rightwing of the Republican Party is the butt of several funny lines, and knowing but funny references to Paris of the 20s (though I admit I had to look up who Djuna Barnes was). The film's message - that the past is a nostalgic illusion and we must live for today - is hardly subtle or original but definitely something we could all do with being reminded about from time to time.<br /><br />On the other hand, there are plenty of flaws. The opening montage goes on far too long. I got it: we're in Paris! The relationship between Gil and Inez is too antagonistic to be credible. And since this is a concept movie, there's no real resolution to it; it can't go anywhere, so Woody just ends it with a downpour.<br /><br />But these are criticisms that are easy to overlook. It's at its best when Owen Wilson is wandering through Paris of almost a century ago and for this part of the movie alone I'd recommend it. Utterly charming.

B00005YUBL|4.0|There's something truly catchy about these readings. The very opening of Schumann's 1st Symphony, catches us off guard with its power and dignity. Booming strings and whacking timpani from the Staatskapelle Dresden take their place in a musical landscape that seeks to reach fulfillment by sheer might. For Sawallisch, these are big creations that thrive when treated big.<br /><br />All the same, Sawallisch is predominately a classicist. Romantic excesses have no place in Sawallisch's world. We witness breathtaking displays of orchestral playing but Sawallisch has a very tight grip. Ultimately, these are literalist interpretations, ones that achieve their success through strict adherence to the score. These are performances that represent a bygone era of intensity, where conductors would lose their temper, breaking batons and violin bows (not that Sawallisch himself is a violent individual).<br /><br />I'm not sure how to rank these discs, because as much as enjoy the fire, I miss Schumann's sunny lyricism. Instead of letting us hear Schumann's freshness and abandon, the symphonies are transformed into solid creations, almost to the point of being statuary. Is anything gained by avoiding reference to the qualities that defined Schumann as a man, his wandering mind with a boundless nervous energy that ruined his fingers and later his mind? Other conductors find more emotion in these masterpieces; Bernstein and Harnoncourt, while hardly similar, are both gripping.<br /><br />In the end, the decision must lie with the listener, as there as much to attract us, only some people, including myself, ask for more flexibility.

B000BYCAJE|5.0|The first time I heard this record in 1969 I was a freshman in high school and I was nervously experiencing my first stint at an overnight camp, Camp Falcon in Carrolton, Ohio, with a bunch of fellas I'd never met before. Marrakesh Express comes on the radio or the tape player and to this day in 2012, I'm back in the cabin listening with my new friends, wondering who these guys were and why we just kept singing this funky new tune. Sure, we had AM Radio with Tommy Roe, The Archies, Steam, Zager & Evans and other red hot one hit wonder bands around the time, but this record, and these three voices, stuck in my mind like glue. From the intense joy of listening to this remarkable record, we all moved on to the electric energy of Woodstock, then the overdone, overlong Four Way Street, to up & down solo careers, sub-par CDs, bad life stories and everything else... but it all started with this amazing recording. David Crosby is right that very few records come along that you can enjoy for hours after 20 years, or now, over 40 years, but this record is still fresh. Among the highlights; the remastered version brings Stills organ and other musical skills to the front on so many tunes, and Helplessly Hoping becomes the most beautiful harmonizing event ever recorded on the planet. While the initial CD sounds fine, this is a little bit finer. The extra cuts are OK but the value lies in the original material, no question. I'm not made out of money, but I've gotten my value, sonically and emotionally, from the three copies of the Vinyl I had, the original CD with less than stellar liner notes, and now this updated package. And this is such unforgettable music I honestly feel like I just bought a new CD! Even with the incriminating evidence of Bree's conversation with Ben, \\"the people\\" are just going to drop all charges in a snap? Even 1 of those things would be enough to slander your ability to run for office. Secondly, that union between Trip & her was so contrived. It felt forced and just a desperate attempt to give her a man at the last minute. After her long track record I think it would have been beautiful to see how she has realized she doesn't need to be co-dependent and to focus on her career instead of getting laid. It would have been more suitable to leave her as the happy independent woman with the world as her oyster rather than Susan. Finally, we've only known this guy for 3 episodes...why would Marc Cherry find a need to do this?<br /><br />LYNETTE- If at gunpoint I had to pick the most heart warming moment...it'd be a stretch, but I say it was the scene with Tom & Lynette. Very sweet, but by no means a tear-jerker. How funny that she only said like 3 lines and it was Tom who took it away. Bree didn't seem like the most appropriate person to turn to? Your supposed best friend, knows all about cooking for the masses/croissants & french pastries, etc. Not to mention the fact that Bree was the head of her own company and managed to run said company until it was taken from her. How befitting for Katherine to help her friend get her foot back in the game while accomplishing her own plans. A love interest is not what Bree needed...a new start and chance is what was in order. Instead Lynette was her first choice, a woman who never ran a company but was vice president of a marketing firm. She knows about advertising and maybe pizza establishments, not corporate food service & delicacies!<br /><br />EDIE- Marc Cherry tried everything in his power to have his audience forget Edie Britt. To the point where it was so apparent and obvious that she was blatantly excluded from the final scene. Ultimately his plan backlashed though, instead his efforts further fueled the fan's memories of this beloved character who aptly noticed her absence. So in wanting us to forget & ignore, everyone did the complete opposite! If he truly wanted to keep his word and make an \\"homage to ALL the characters of wisteria lane\\", this capable writer could have found a creative way to incorporate the major characters who are missed without necessarily needing the actors to be there themselves. With this scenario he chose to keep, it limited our choices as to how to do characters such as Edie Britt justice. In light of the legal matters off screen and this creative route he forced upon us, the viewer's hands were tied in terms of this finale moment & Edie Britt. Rather Ellie the drug-dealer from Season 5 gets a mention...rude, rude, rude!<br /><br />KATHERINE- Dana Delany specifically requested to Marc (and was very vocal in interviews) about wanting to keep Katherine a lesbian if she were to return. Is there a reason why he ignored her input? If getting Julie Benz as Robin was a problem, couldn't we have said she just stayed back in Paris to hold down the fort? There you go, instant happy ending for Katherine! Instead, he turned her into a wishy washy, flip-flopping hazbian who becomes rich, snobby, & stuck up. How is this a great ending to a character of so many layers who starred in this series for 3 years? BTW, I love how they didn't bother to tell her Mike died...this didn't cross any of the girl's minds?<br /><br />OTHER CHARACTERS- It was a long time coming, but Renee finally grew on me by the finale with that hilarious moment when she snapped at Ben in the sweaty wedding dress. Although it was quite random and unexplained why Julie (of all people) was in the limo...I don't think I ever saw those 2 interact. Meanwhile the maid of honor was missing, but I do have to commend Renee for all she did in the courtroom. If I was in her shoes and my partner was being deported, I would have done the same. These women were such flaky \\"friends\\" to her. I'm sad the viewers didn't get to find out what happened to Bob & Lee, a quick little reference of their daughter would have sufficed. Unfortunately this was not to be, Marc Cherry figured we were more interested in watching Trip chase after Bree and buying old record players. BTW, where was MJ during the wedding/childbirth moments? Where was Lynette's baby? Her speech when she declared \\"to remembering...\\" is very ill-timed when it seems all the housewives can't seem to remember they have young ones in the family that need to be tended to. As an afterthought, what happened to Chuck's partner who wouldn't rest until Bree was behind bars? His cries for vengeance were only the cliffhanger for every episode in the latter half of this season, then he gives up? And was the drama with the girl moving into Susan's house absolutely crucial?<br /><br />Sadly, Marc Cherry claims he's had this well thought-out ending sequence since the premise of the series was initially created years before being picked up by ABC. If that's the case, I am highly disappointed. So many speedy conclusions, all too convenient, the rest was riddled with inconsistencies, holes in the plot,<br />unaddressed/forgotten issues, dropped storylines, and improper farewells to women we've gotten so invested in. While the attempt was duly noted, this series ending will never stand in the likes of the hardhitters of iconic endings like the ones mentioned at the beginning of this dissertation. And no amount of cliffhangers from characters we're never gonna get to know in the last 5 minutes of the show will distract from what was sorely lacking. Friend & family ask why I'm so disillusioned by this and can't just let it be, it's because I've gotten so involved. The response I get is \\"it's just entertainment, it's just a show, Marc Cherry & his writers simply stopped trying\\". To add insult to injury, we know these absurdly over-payed actress' couldn't care less, they've got their careers and future to worry about and in the meantime are laughing all the way to the bank!!!! So I'm glad everyone involved with the network ends up with a happy ending, while the actual story (the artistry) and the viewers who kept them alive during the low periods are left with a piece of their hearts ripped out...and not in the poignant sort of way (more like the Hollywood uses you and spits you out sorta way). So thank you for all the good times Desperate Housewives, there are parts of you I will miss and most certainly the rest that I'm most relieved to get rid of =(.

B0006HC0I2|5.0|Hey, I have been touting crime noir films for the past couple of years so why not review, as here with Jean Gabin's 1954 Touchez Pas au Grisbi, a French crime noir in honor of, well, the name of the genre. And a later generation of French directors who went crazy for Hollywood gangster epics in such films as \\"Breathless\\" and Don't Shoot The Piano Player. Especially when said this crime noir stars Jean Gabin, last reviewed here in a very different film, Children Of Paradise.<br /><br />Well, let's cut to the chase (literally, as this plot unfolds). It seems that long- time crime boss Max (Gabin) has pulled a caper (heist, okay) to set himself and his confederate Riton up for a well-deserved retirement from the rackets. And everything was going along just fine until old buddy Riton got a loose tongue over some show girl (played by a very young Jean Moreau) and spilled his guts out to her about how he could keep her in clover. Problem is that young showgirls are as fickle and calculating as any other woman mixed up with bad actuarial table criminals and she has another crime boss on the hook, one Angelo. Naturally she mentions the loot to Angelo and sets off an explosion of maneuvers by him to get the kale, and by Max to keep it.<br /><br />The cat and mouse of this pair drives the rest of the movie with old Max showing one and all why he was (and is) the king of the hill, even if he may have lost a step or two. Angelo's big mistake (besides thinking that Max was over the hill and easy pickings) was kidnapping Riton, an act that set Max on a fight to the finish. See the film to get the details of that fight to the finish. What is important though is the use by the director here of the many tough guy moves made by Hollywood gangsters in the heyday 1930s including a few off-hand beatings of opponent gangsters to get information, a few off-hand slaps at show girls (by Gabin of all people but that is part of being a boss and no chump), and a car chase, natch complete withmachine guns ta-ta-ta-ing. 1594200106|5.0|'Few things leave a deeper mark on a reader than the first book that finds its way into his heart. Those first images, the echo of words we think we have left behind, accompany us throughout our lives and sculpt a palace in our memory to which, sooner or later - no matter how many books we read, how many worlds we discover, or how much we learn to forget - we will return.'<br /><br />The story begins in the early summer of 1945 in Barcelona, in the wake of the Spanish Civil War, when ten-year-old Daniel Sempere is taken to the ancient, cavernous Cemetery of Forgotten Books for the first time by his antiquarian bookseller father. From the hundreds of thousands of books contained within the endless corridors of the labyrinthine bibliographic mausoleum, Daniel must choose one book to adopt; he will, he is told, be that book's guardian for the rest of his life. He chooses The Shadow of the Wind by an obscure Spanish novelist called Juli&aacute;n Carax - a choice that will change his life forever.<br /><br />Captivated by the book, Daniel becomes intrigued by its enigmatic author. Over the years that follow, as he grows from a young boy into an awkward adolescent, he uncovers various clues about Carax and attempts to build a picture of the man. He is told that Carax was killed in Barcelona at the beginning of the civil war and discovers that his is the only surviving copy of The Shadow of the Wind; a sinister, faceless character who smells of burned paper and calls himself La&iacute;n Coubert, the name of a character created by Carax, has been going around burning every book by Carax that he can find and Daniel's copy survived only because it had been kept safe in the Cemetery of Forgotten Books.<br /><br />With the help of his frequently entertaining sidekick Ferm&iacute;n Romero de Torres (a man with secrets of his own), Daniel begins to piece together the truth about the tragic events of Juli&aacute;n Carax's life, events which appear unnervingly similar to those taking place in his own life. In the process, he stirs up emotions amongst those who once knew Carax and attracts the decidedly dangerous attention of the vengeful and sadistic police inspector (and ex-Francoist torturer and assassin) Francisco Javier Fumero, whose quest for vengeance threatens the lives of Daniel and those close to him.<br /><br />It's difficult to go into any further detail without giving too much away, so I won't. What I will say is that this is a fabulous, captivating book; a book about books, which is the very best kind of book for a bibliophile like me. There are plot twists and turns aplenty; there are characters to love and hate, and there is tragedy, romance and humour (especially when Ferm&iacute;n Romero de Torres is about). Once I began reading, I really didn't want to stop. This truly is an outstanding book.. B005LZW8FO|4.0|To set a few things straight, \\"Another Earth\\" IS a sci-fi film in the strictest sense of the word, but do not go into it thinking of it that way.  It depends upon a suspension of disbelief but let's delay that discussion that for a moment.<br /><br />The plot is pretty straightforward: Rhoda, a bright, young girl headed to MIT leaves a party and takes her eyes off the road because her car radio announces a mysterious new planet is visible in the night sky.  Just as she spots it she slams into another car, killing a man's pregnant wife and child and leaving him in a coma.<br /><br />Four years later she leaves prison a ruined young woman, barely able to speak to anyone.  She takes a job as a janitor at a high school, probably fulfilling some testament to the ruination of her own educational dreams.  And when she tracks down the man whose family she destroyed, she flubs that, too, and ends up lying to him and telling him she's from a cleaning service, and she cleans his home every week for him.<br /><br />That's mostly it, but at the same time it isn't it at all.  So much goes on in terms of mood, posture, the length of the shot, and almost always the ever-enlarging aura of the other planet, one that seems to be mirroring us in every way.<br /><br />And that's where the really interesting dilemma comes in: could an earth that is SO much like ours--one that very well might have been EXACTLY like ours until the moment we (and they) spotted one another--be infinitesimally different ENOUGH that Rhoda might be able to escape the horror of her life, and the wrong that she did that night, the very night we spotted each other?<br /><br />I want to tell you more, but I worry so of spoiling it, which I will not do.  But there are three important things you should know about this film:<br /><br />1.  Don't dwell on the picky questions that sci-fi people tend to dwell over and could ruin your enjoyment.  Don't wonder how this planet escaped our detection until now.  Don't worry about how it affects our tides, or orbits, or atmosphere, or any of the little nitpicky things that would get in your way.  Think of the sci-fi in a less demanding sense of, say, a \\"Twilight Zone\\" or \\"Outer Limits\\" TV episode.  It is absolutely necessary for the other earth to be there...it's just not something that demands scientific specificity.<br /><br />2.  This is an independent film: low budget, hand-held cameras, occasional fuzzy shots, etc.  But it is not bothersome or \\"Blair Witch\\"-y. B00018D3RI|5.0|I bought the DVD's for S&H a couple of years ago, and have recently been re-watching them (along with re-runs of the show currently showing on RTV), and I really am amazed how well this show holds up so many years later.  You can see the recycled cop storylines throughout the years from cop show to cop show, but S&H was so unique in it allowing it's 2 lead characters to honestly and truly care about each other.  Possibly TV's first true (and strongest) 'bromance' as they call it today, the relationship between Starsky and Hutch was the glue the held the show together.  As a fan of the show, you have to be commited to that relationship as well in order to completely enjoy it for all that it is.<br /><br />The show was also important for it's Captain.  It wasn't common place in the 1970's to see a black man in a position of power on TV. Captain Dobey not only fit that role, but did it with class and honesty with a fun streak tossed in.  He was respected by those he commanded (he cared about them as well) and he was a well rounded family man.  He was a much needed and much welcomed part of TV history.<br /><br />S&H was also unique in that it tackled difficult issues without backing away from them with easy answers.  Drug addiction (\\"The Fix\\" is easily one of most powerful and controversial episodes of the show), homosexuality (in a brilliant and powerful episode called \\"Death in a Different Place\\"), alcoholism, violent crimes against children, death of loved ones, etc.  It never made light of the issues and always treated them with a respect that one would not expect from a show of the 1970's.<br /><br />As someone else wrote in a review down below, you won't see a show like this ever again on television.  Sure it was corny at times and there are a few episodes that just fall flat (what show doesn't have those?), but in the end, it's a show that is not afraid of itself and one that cared about the characters and their relationships above all else.

6302787068|4.0|***This review may contain spoilers***<br /><br />  It's hardly a mystery why the \\"Aladdin\\" myth attracted Disney's animation crew back in 1992.  This timeless story boasts everything: exotic locales, a scrappy hero, flamboyant characters, opulent royalty, giddy romance, despicable and devious villains, and, most tellingly, the endless possibilities of magic as epitomized by the genie.  However, as Disney's animators crafted this dynamic spectacle of a movie, I think they must have been concerned that one of \\"Aladdin\\"'s greatest assets -the protean, boundlessly energetic, and fast-talking Genie, as voiced by the inimitable comic pantomiming maelstrom Robin Williams- might ironically turn out to be one of its greatest liabilities.  In the Genie, we have a dominating, omnipotent character who can seemingly do anything.  So, once Aladdin gets possession of the Genie's lamp, isn't the movie over?  Just three wishes, and it's happily ever after.<br /><br />  Besides, doesn't Aladdin succeed solely because of the Genie?  You may recall that Aladdin (voice of Scott Weinger) promises to free the unbridled genie with his third wish.  However, after the Genie makes him a fabulously wealthy prince on the verge of wooing the Sultan's (Douglas Seale) alluring daughter Princess Jasmine (Linda Larkin) toward matrimony, Aladdin wants to renege on the deal because he feels he cannot continue this ultimate charade without the Genie's continued help.  Understandably, this puts the Genie in a funk and leads to a series of plot twists that place the Genie under the control of the ruthlessly ambitious Sultan's vizier (consultant) Jafar (Jonathan Freeman).  It seems that Aladdin goes from hero back to hopeless zero.<br /><br />  But hold on thearrrr, as Quick Draw McGraw would say, Disney shows us that Aladdin is not such a hopeless nothing.  From the beginning of the film, where we see the penniless orphan and his pilfering simian accomplice Abu (Frank Welker) acrobatically elude the Sultan's security guards led by the burly, irascible Razoul (Jim Cummings), we notice that Aladdin is nimble, clever, street smart, plucky and adaptable.  Even after being trapped in the Cave of Wonders by a disguised Jafar (who needs Aladdin to obtain the magic lamp) and finding the lamp, Aladdin tricks the trickster Genie into freeing him without wasting a wish.  Furthermore, even the Genie's vast power has its limits.  Besides servitude to whoever has the lamp, the Genie informs Aladdin that he has no power over death or love.  In other words, he can't be an assassin, or the Messiah, or Cupid.  If Aladdin wants to woo Jasmine, he ultimately has to do it on his own.  And when Jafar exploits the Genie to become a sultan and sorcerer and uses his power to neutralize his friends, Aladdin again has to find it within himself to defeat Jafar on his own.  Instead of false princely status, he uses his courage, sly wit and Jafar's voracious desire for ultimate magic to bring him down.<br /><br />  So you see, \\"Aladdin\\" belies its ethereal, insubstantial structure and supernatural fantasy elements to remind us that, after all, even if you have true friends to help you along, the true magic to make your life better lies within you.tan's security guards led by the burly, irascible Razoul (Jim Cummings), we notice that Aladdin is nimble, clever, street smart, plucky and adaptable. 0385334206|2.0|Part memoir, part novel, part experimental meta-narrative, \\"Breakfast of Champions\\" is a jumbled assortment of literary devices and imagination rolled into a book peppered with felt-tip drawings by the author. To give it a plot is to loosely give you an idea of what's going on in the book though really it only makes up for a third of it or so: Kilgore Trout, pulp novelist, is invited to a literary festival at the behest of his only fan, a wealthy eccentric called Eliot Rosewater, in the same town where Dwayne Hoover, a Pontiac salesman and Burger Chef franchise owner, is slowly losing his mind and is destined for a fateful encounter with the eponymous Trout.<br /><br />I quite like Kurt Vonnegut's work. \\"Cat's Cradle\\" was pretty good, while his posthumously published \\"Armageddon in Retrospect\\" was enjoyable as well, though I've started and stopped \\"Slaughterhouse 5\\" maybe twice now because I've found it unreadable and difficult to get through. I made it through this book, one of his more popular and well known works, but I found it not without its own problems.<br /><br />What's annoying the most is the random nature of Vonnegut's writing. When he writes his fictional narrative he diverges into stark, almost patronising, moments of explication where he has to inform you what something he's just mentioned is. For example what the United States is, what capitalism is, a moment in history, and then an explanation followed by a drawing of something irrelevant like a t-shirt or a finger. Then he goes down another sidetrack into a moment of memoir about his own life before getting back to the (increasingly boring) story. Then he addresses the reader, informing them when he is writing fiction and that everything he says and does when doing so is made up (duh) and telling the reader when he's writing non-fiction. Then he tells you what a burger is by drawing a cow and then a burger. It's all so frustratingly pointless.<br /><br />Vonnegut, rather tellingly, writes at the start about recently turning 50 and how this book is a way of getting all of the junk out of his head, and the book ends with the words \\"make me young, make me young!\\" so maybe the novel is a reaction to growing old/a kind of late mid-life crisis. Or the rather wearying and scatological form of storytelling in the book might be a reaction to his fears of mental illness which plagued his mother, eventually causing her to take her life, and which dogged Vonnegut throughout his own life, leading to his suicide attempts.<br /><br />The patchwork nature of the book belies the claims of other fans of his work that it is a masterpiece, which I contest. The fact that the book reads like Vonnegut bouncing around the walls of his brain does not make this anything more than what it reads like - a mess. The characters are barely there, the story is irrelevant to Vonnegut's seemingly sole purpose of showing how a novelist is like a puppet master - the meta angle - which, reading it in the 21st century, is quite dull. It's also quite a miserable novel in tone with Vonnegut labelling doing a job without purpose \\"suicide\\" and boiling down descriptions of male characters to penis size and female characters to breast/wait size.<br /><br />The book comes across as a hodge-podge of non-sequiturs, scraps of ideas, half-baked stories and a lot of Vonnegut talking to reader about how his book doesn't really hang together, how pretentious the role of the author is, and how doomed we all are. It's like reading a book by a mentally deranged bore standing on a box pointing out the bleeding obvious. \\"Breakfast of Champions\\" is a long way from being a masterpiece and is quite dull to read as well as being one of Vonnegut's least interesting books.art about recently turning 50 and how this book is a way of getting all of the junk out of his head, and the book ends with the words \\"make me young, make me young!\\" so maybe the novel is a reaction to growing old/a kind of late mid-life crisis. The fact that the book reads like Vonnegut bouncing around the walls of his brain does not make this anything more than what it reads like - a mess. It's also quite a miserable novel in tone with Vonnegut labelling doing a job without purpose \\"suicide\\" and boiling down descriptions of male characters to penis size and female characters to breast/wait size.<br /><br />The book comes across as a hodge-podge of non-sequiturs, scraps of ideas, half-baked stories and a lot of Vonnegut talking to reader about how his book doesn't really hang together, how pretentious the role of the author is, and how doomed we all are. 0743244257|5.0|In Part One of COSMOPOLIS, DeLillo primarily examines the lives of two characters. The first and dominant character is Eric Packer, a young hedge fund manager worth tens of billions of dollars, who risks the complete loss of his fortune and fund if the Yen rises. Eric is at the nexus of capital and technology and believes that he can spot and profit from movements in money and information that are unseen by others. Says Eric: \\"There's an order at some deep level...A pattern that wants to be seen.\\" Says Kinski, his Chief of Theory: \\"A consciousness such as yours, hypermaniacal, may have contact points beyond the general perception.\\"<br /><br />Meanwhile, the second major character introduced in Part One is Benno Levin, a former trader and generic laborer at Packer Capital, who lost his job. Thinks Benno: \\"There are great themes running through my mind. The themes of loneliness and human discard. The theme of who do I have when there's no one left.\\"<br /><br />In Part Two, DeLillo examines the tropism toward destruction and death in these two characters. In Eric's case, this tropism manifests as his huge bet on the Yen gradually sours. Then, DeLillo seems to ask: If a man like Eric can't win, will he attempt to take the system down with him? In contrast, the plight of Benno, who was trashed by Eric's cyber-capitalism, represents a different question. Namely: Is there anything a victim of capitalism can do that actually rights the ruthless wrongs inherent to the system?<br /><br />COSMOPOLIS is a surrealistic satire that is tersely hilarious. It begins with the sleepless Eric in his 48-room apartment near the FDR Drive, where he enjoys an atrium, screening room, and shark tank, and ends in an abandoned tenement near the West Side Highway where the characters have no roles to play in life. Along this riches-to-rags continuum, DeLillo supplies innumerable instances of amazing writing and insight. The man, truly, expands what you see. Here, for example, is Eric during his confrontation with Benno.<br /><br />\\"He'd always wanted to become quantum dust, transcending his body mass, the soft tissue over the bones, the muscle and fat. The ideas was to live outside the given limits, in a chip, on a disk, as data, in whirl, in radiant spin, a consciousness saved from void...It would be the master thrust of cyber-capital, to extend the human experience toward infinity as a medium for corporate growth and investment, for the accumulation of profits and vigorous reinvestment.\\"<br /><br />This is not my favorite DeLillo novel. But COSMOPOLIS has great insight and style and is eerily prescient about risk taking on Wall Street, which, like the hubristic Eric Packer, is clearly willing to risk and embrace economic meltdown for big paydays.<br /><br />Recommended.confrontation with Benno.<br /><br />\\"He'd always wanted to become quantum dust, transcending his body mass, the soft tissue over the bones, the muscle and fat. 0385737955|5.0|The Plot:<br /><br />Thomas wakes up trapped in a dark box, with no recollection of his past, his identity (other than his name), or his purpose. Soon, the Box is opened by a group of teenage boys he doesn't recognize, and Thomas emerges from the Box into a bizarre world which is surrounded by huge concrete walls on all sides. No one can tell him who he is or why he is there, because they all started in the Box too.<br /><br />The boys tell him that the he is in the Glade. And outside the Glade, through the huge doors in the concrete walls, lies the Maze. They have formed a small yet functional society within the Glade, surviving until the time when one of them can find a way out. And the only way they can conceive of to escape is to solve the Maze.<br /><br />Every day, the doors open. The Runners go out, searching for an exit. Every night, the Runners return, the doors close, and terrifying monsters prowl the corridors of the Maze. To be trapped in the Maze at night is to guarantee a horrific death.<br /><br />The Runners have been searching for an exit from the Maze for years, but have never found a solution. But Thomas feels an inexplicable pull to become a Runner. And although he can't explain why, he thinks he can solve the Maze.<br /><br />My Thoughts:<br /><br />Holy cow, people. This book was insane. I was completely riveted from page 1, something that rarely happens. Even with books I completely adore, it normally takes me a chapter or two to immerse myself in the world of the book. But with The Maze Runner, I was in that Box with Thomas. I was confused and uncomfortable and determined to make sense of the situation.<br /><br />As the book went on, I, like Thomas, was completely perplexed and frustrated by the mystery of the Maze and why they were trapped in the Glade. But James Dashner had an uncanny ability to predict what I was going to ask, and then have Thomas ask that very question. For example, when they're explaining that getting trapped in the Maze at night is a death sentence, I wondered if they'd attempted climbing the walls. Then Thomas asks, \\"Why don't you just climb the walls?\\" So although I couldn't figure out for the life of me how they were going to escape the Maze or why we were there, I was frustrated with the characters instead of at the characters.<br /><br />The pacing of this book was excellent. While the action doesn't really kick up until about 1/3 of the way into the book, I still felt my adrenaline pumping right away just because of the strangeness of the situation. There was suspense simply in not knowing why any of this was happening. Then, once Thomas gets his bearings a bit better, the real action picked up. So basically, I felt like I was having heart palpitations for the majority of this book. In a good way.<br /><br />The characters were also well developed. While not every Glader was fully fleshed-out, the main ones all had their own personalities and layers. I felt like I knew these kids, and I found myself cheering for some and rolling my eyes with Thomas at others.<br /><br />Even the dialogue, which I sometimes find really annoying in sci-fi/dystopian books when the author feels the need to throw in a bunch of made-up \\"future\\" slang, somehow felt natural in this book. Maybe it's because Thomas draws attention to it almost immediately, saying it sounds weird and foreign. Since it's acknowledged in the book, I accepted it and moved on. Eventually, it began to sound natural, to me and to Thomas.<br /><br />Bottom line: I thought this book was amazing. It's not for everyone. It's got an extremely high \\"weird\\" factor. And the suspense that kept me on the edge of my seat for the entire book may not be what others are looking for. But for me, I completely and totally adored this book. I couldn't put it down (literally. I read the whole thing in a day, something I was not planning on doing). It's unlike anything else I've ever read. My only complaint is that my library doesn't have Book2 available RIGHT NOW.2 available RIGHT NOW.

B005LAIHQS|4.0|THE PLOT: J (Will Smith) and K (Tommy Lee Jones) encounter troubles when a dangerous alien criminal, Boris (Jemaine Clement), escapes from his moon-based prison and goes back in time to kill K before the agent can capture and imprison him. When K (and all of his influence on the world) dissolves away, only J remembers him, which sends him back in time to 1969 to save his partner.<br /><br />WHY YOU MIGHT LIKE IT: The formula hasn't changed much. Will Smith does a really good Will Smith impression, and most of his wisecracks are amusing. There are some standard \\"fish out of water\\" jokes when K goes back to the less-than-tolerant 60's, but that is far less obtrusive than it could be. The weapons, the creatures, the special effects, and the slap-happy plotting are great, chewy fun, like a B-movie with an A-movie budget. In other words, this movie walks pretty much the same lines as its predecessors, and it does so with a comforting and addictive flair.<br /><br />WHY YOU MIGHT NOT LIKE IT: If you are a person who thinks more than he feels, you'll very quickly notice some gaping holes in the plot, most of which yawn wide open in the third act. Movies that involve time travel usually have to dance around shaky logic, but this film doesn't even bother with that. At a key moment in the plot -- the final climax -- J uses his time travel device in a way that doesn't even TRY to make sense. The screenwriters must have known this, because the scene moves too fast for any viewer to really stop and understand it. Then the story very quickly shifts into heartstrings territory, providing the audience with a conclusion that is so sweetly sad that it is nearly emotional enough to distract from the fact that it, too, makes absolutely no sense whatsoever.<br /><br />Adding to the story's befuddlement is the addition of an alien character named Griffin. A fifth-dimensional being, Griffin is able to view and exist in multiple timelines at the same time, making him both omnipresent and omniscient. He is essentially God, if God were an effeminate voyeur with a cold. As a God-like character, Griffin acts as a double-barreled deus ex machina. He provides the story with more than a few easy outs, but the solutions he provide raise further questions that the movie is in too much of a hurry to bother answering.<br /><br />It's all in good silly fun, most people will tell you, and they're right. It's quite enjoyable if you're willing to suspend your disbelief a little longer than you might normally, even for a movie about time travelling aliens. Fastidious viewers will find a lot here to rant about, but the kid in you might find enough reason to shrug off the flaws and just enjoy the ride.mniscient. 0393064476|5.0|The poem by Lucretius, \\"On the Nature of Things\\", does not strike one today as being of any particular interest, beyond \\"style\\" and a certain aesthetic appeal.  We live in a world today that has become, in its own way, very \\"atomistic\\" and \\"Epicurean\\", and so the intellectual slant of the poem does not impress.  This is often the way with things:  They can appear to us to be very plain and ordinary, while their tiny \\"signal\\" from the deep past is worthy of greater attention.  This is what Prof. Greenblatt hopes to achieve, to amplify this tiny signal so that we appreciate its power and beauty in the course of human history.<br /><br />Like much of Roman antiquity, it proved to be a fragile remnant that was nearly lost.  Prof. Greenblatt tells a story about its recovery by an Italian book collector in the early Renaissance, someone who was both a humanist and deeply involved in (secular) aspects of the Catholic Church.  The tale revolves around the strangeness of the poem and the strangeness of the times.  Atomism was a perceived threat to Catholicism on the one hand due to its confrontation with Aristotelian concepts that Catholics had fully woven into their doctrine, and Epicurean ideas were threatening because the focused on the importance to seek pleasure, over the suffering and misery that was Christ's lot.  Many principles  discussed in the poem were contrary(and perceived as threatening) to Christian dogma, as it was understood in the early Renaissance.  Prof. Greenblatt suggests that not only did the modern world evolve to become more in accord with the ideas of the poem, but the poem, in itself, had some positive influence on this evolution.  The thoroughly-researched book that has resulted displays a very fine historical portrait, one that leads us not only to appreciate the intellectual content of Lucretius' poem, but also its erotic beauty.  In addition, I personally appreciated very much his vignettes of people whose lives were somewhat influenced by the poem following its recovery, perhaps, most interestingly, by his discussions of Poggio, the book collector who found a copy of the poem in a monastery.  The poem resulted in much less consternation and censorship than might have been expected, due to the general lack of availability and translations in the years immediately following its recovery.<br /><br />To provide a better perspective on his presentation, let us examine one of the chapters in his book as representative.  Let's examine Chapter 8, \\"The Way Things Are\\".  The first paragraph sets the stage:  \\"\\"On the Nature of Things\\" is not an easy read.  Totaling 7,400 lines, it is written in hexameters, the standard unrhymed six-beat lines in which Latin poets like Virgil and Ovid, imitating Homer's Greek, cast their epic poetry.  Divided into six untitled books, the poem yokes together moments of intense lyrical beauty, philosophical meditations on religion, pleasure and death, and complex theories of the physical world, the evolution of human societies, the perils and joys of sex, and the nature of disease.  The language is often knotty and difficult, the syntax complex, and the overall ambition astoundingly high.\\"  This suggests how this poem came to the Christian world in an \\"alien\\" or \\"otherworldly\\" way, and the chapter concludes with:  \\"Certainly almost every one of the poem's key principles was an abomination to right-thinking Christian orthodoxy.  But the poetry was compellingly, seductively beautiful.  And we can see with hallucinatory vividness what at least one Italian, later in the fifteenth century, made of them:  we have only to look at Boticelli's great painting of Venus, ravishingly beautiful, emerging from the restless matter of the sea.\\"<br /><br />It is odd that a poem could be regarded as intellectually dangerous, in itself, because as an isolated remnant of an ancient culture, it did not come to the Europeans as a coherent threat.  Nevertheless, one recognizes that we are all children of imagination to varying degrees, and, merely based on the way our imaginations operate, exposure to fantasy or new ideas can pose challenges.  In the arrival of Lucretius' book after submergence in the past, did the poem pose a danger of encouraging atheism?  \\"...Lucretius was in fact not an atheist.  He believed that the gods existed.  But he also believed that, by virtue of being gods, they could not possibly be concerned with human beings or with anything we do.  Divinity by its very nature, he thought, must enjoy eternal life and peace entirely untouched by any suffering or disturbance and indifferent to human actions.\\"  No, he was not an atheist, but his \\"message\\" was not a welcome one for Christians.<br /><br />\\"These views were certainly contrary to Poggio's own Christian faith and would have led any contemporary who espoused them into the most serious trouble.  But by themselves, encountered in a pagan text, they were not likely to trigger great alarm.  Poggio could have told himself, as did nome later sympathetic readers of \\"On the Nature of Things\\", that the brilliant ancient poet simply intuited the emptiness of pagan beliefs and hence the absurdity of sacrifices to gods who did not in fact exist.  Lucretius, after, all, had the misfortune of living shortly before the coming of the Messiah.  Had he been born a century later, he would have had the opportunity of learning the truth.\\"  In other words, we see in the recovery of this poem, a parallel with modern views, but perhaps just one small influence leading to the modern world.  This was the significance of the Renaissance and humanism.<br /><br />In the first and final paragraphs of the chapter, Prof. Greenblatt has framed, in part, the theme of the chapter.  Now he is exploring it.  He has focused on its intellectual content.  He emphasizes in the beginning the disturbing aspect of the poem for Christians, but what is the overall challenge of the poem, its \\"key principles\\"?  In the latter part of the chapter, he lists these, discussing them slightly, and we are able to grasp the startlingly modern tone of these principles, and how this poem could have served, in part, as an inspiration for our modern age.  Here is the list of the main points, which are written, in the book, in boldface type, and which Prof. Greenblatt wishes to emphasize are present within the body of the poem, emerging as part of the intellectual content:<br />1.  Everything is made of invisible particles.<br />2. The elementary particles of matter - \\"the seeds of the things\\" - are eternal.<br />3. The elementary particles are infinite in number but limited in shape and size.<br />4. All particles are in motion in an infinite void.<br />5. Everything comes into being as a result of the swerve.  (\\"The swerve ... is only the most minimal motions ... But it is enough to set off a ceaseless chain of collisions.\\")<br />6. The swerve is the source of free will.  (\\"Lucretius' image is the split second on the race track after the starting gate is opened, before the straining horses, frantically eager to move, can actually propel their bodies forward.  That split second is the thrilling spectacle of a mental act bidding a mass of matter into motion.\\")<br />7. Nature ceaselessly experiments.  (\\"There is no single moment of origin, no mythic scene of creation.\\")<br />8. The universe was not created for or about humans.<br />9. Humans are not unique.  (\\"They are part of a much larger material process that links them not only to all other life forms but to inorganic matter as well.\\")<br />10. Human society began not in a Golden Age of tranquility and plenty, but in a primitive battle for survival.\\")<br />11. The soul dies.  (\\"The human soul is made of the same material as the human body.\\")<br />12. There is no afterlife.<br />13. All organized religions are superstitious delusions.<br />14. Religions are invariably cruel.<br />15. There are no angels, demons, or ghosts.<br />16. The highest goal of human life is the enhancement of pleasure and the reduction of pain.<br />17. The greatest obstacle to pleasure is not pain; it is delusion.<br />18. Understanding the nature of things generates deep wonder.<br />Of course, this gives only briefly, a flavor of a single chapter.  The other chapters are woven together, connecting with this one, in an overall and complex harmony.<br /><br />Prof. Greenblatt has done an admirable job in writing an engaging, entertaining, and informative history associated with the early Renaissance, and focusing on the recovery of Lucretius' ancient poem and its consequences.  I feel that the scholarship of the work is very high, so that we are not only treated to an interesting story, but one built carefully on historical accuracy.  I can recommend this work highly, especially as Prof. Greenblatt is able to establish a broad perspective for the historical point in time when humanists recovered Lucretius' beautiful poem.ter, he lists these, discussing them slightly, and we are able to grasp the startlingly modern tone of these principles, and how this poem could have served, in part, as an inspiration for our modern age. The holster cannot be clipped or unclipped with any degree of ease.<br /><br />It these restrictions don't disturb you, then this holster might work out. However, I also own a Nebo holster that cost less and proves to be much more practical. That would be my first choice.

B00001OH7U|5.0|There seems to be a major resurgence of interest in 1970s music as today's 15-25 year olds rediscover music from that decade. I grew up in the 70s and my friends' teenaged sons are now frequently asking me about 70s bands and looking for lesser known bands and albums from that era.<br /><br />David Bowie can hardly be classified as a \\"lesser known\\" musician but I've discovered that many of the young guys asking me about 70s music have heard of Bowie but they aren't sure which of his many albums to check out first. So to help rectify that situation.......<br /><br />This 1976 release is one of my three personal favourites among all of Bowie's extensive catalogue (the other two are Alladin Sane and Ziggy Stardust) and is one of the essential albums that should be in any respectable collection of 1970s music.<br /><br />Bowie has changed his image and sound repeatedly over the years. This album is from his Thin White Duke period, which started circa 1975 and followed his Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders From Mars phase, which lasted circa 1972-74. If you're not overly familiar with Bowie, this is, by far, the best album of Bowie's Thin White Duke period. If your music collection only has room for a only a few Bowie albums, this should be one of them.<br /><br />I recall seeing an interview with Bowie many years ago in which he said that he had such a bad cocaine habit in 1976 that he didn't remember making this album. It's still a great album in spite of Bowie's cocaine addiction while making it and his inability to remember recording it.<br /><br />For those who aren't overly familiar with Bowie, there are also two albums from his Spiders From Mars period you should check out: Aladdin Sane; and Ziggy Stardust & the Spiders From Mars. Both are great albums, both have survived the test of time and both (IMHO) would easily rate among the 1970s' top 100 albums.<br /><br />Of those two albums, I have always prefered Alladin Sane over Ziggy Stardust so, although I recommend buying both of them, I'd suggest buying Aladdin Sane first if you don't yet own either album.<br /><br />Bottom line: I'd rate Station To Station, Aladdin Sane and Ziggy Stardust as Bowie's best three albums and as essential albums for any serious collection of 1970s music.uying both of them, I'd suggest buying Aladdin Sane first if you don't yet own either album.<br /><br />Bottom line: I'd rate Station To Station, Aladdin Sane and Ziggy Stardust as Bowie's best three albums and as essential albums for any serious collection of 1970s music.

We've known for a while that JM was heading in this direction (California folk/rock ala Neil Young), and I was worried that the album would sound contrived.  It does not, at least to me.  I think this album is what \\"Battle Studies\\" should have been.  I think JM was heading in this direction, the \\"California rock\\" sound, but hadn't fully dived in at that point.  Because of that, I think \\"Battle Studies\\" ended up sounding kind of generic, didn't really have a cohesive direction to it.  I ended up liking some of the songs on it, but not at first listen.  It had to grow on me.<br /><br />This album is great from the first listen.  There is a clear direction to the album.  And it doesn't come off as contrived to me.  It's definitely not \\"Continuum\\", and it's not supposed to be.  It's different in almost every way (lyrically, instrumentally, thematically).  \\"Queen of California\\" is a great opener, and sets the tone for the album well.  It has a James Taylor feel to it (think Mud Slide Slim album).  The outro piano part reminds me of the Allman Brothers \\"Brothers and Sisters\\" era sound with Chuck Leavell on piano.  I say all that, but it doesn't sound like JM is trying to sound like anyone else.  The vocals and lyrics still sound like it's coming from him, not him trying to sound like Neil Young or James Taylor.  That's what I like about this album.  He can wear his influences on his sleeve without it sounding contrived.<br /><br />\\"Age of Worry\\" is a great song, very different and interesting.  It has a classic, grand Americana sound, and an interesting melody.<br /><br />\\"Shadow Days\\" and \\"Something Like Olivia\\" are probably the most radio-ready songs on the album.  Nice, simple, well done songs.  \\"Something Like Olivia\\" doesn't exactly fit with the style of the rest of the album, but it's still a great song.  I really like the guitar sound he gets on it.  You can hear the space between the amp and the mic, hear the space in the studio.<br /><br />\\"Born and Raised\\" has quickly become my favorite song on the album.  Again, he definitely aims for a CSNY sound, but it still sounds honest, sounds like a JM song.  It gets stuck in your head easily, which JM has a knack for doing.  The harmonies by Crosby and Nash are simple, but really add a lot.<br /><br />\\"Walt Grace\\" is also an impressive song.  It's a departure from JM's normal song writing style.  He's usually introspective, writing in the first person, or making general observations on society (\\"Waiting on the World to Change\\", \\"Belief\\").  This is a third-person, story telling song, which I think is a lost art.  Paul McCartney was great at that, writing interesting stories in songs.  Here, JM is able to convey an entire story in 3 simple verses.  Very visual lyrics, and the lyrics fit with the music.<br /><br />The \\"Born and Raised Reprise\\" finishes off the album well, and the bonus track \\"Fool\\" is great as well.  It sounds like it could have been on \\"Continuum\\", but he gives it a folk feel with the harmonica.<br /><br />Again, wonderful album that I haven't been able to stop listening to all week. At some point, I still want JM to make a blistering blues guitar album.  He's too good of a blues guitarist not to.  He still hasn't just let it rip on a studio album yet (though he does on the \\"Where the Light Is\\" live album).  I think the next generation of guitarists needs a new blues album to be inspired by, like JM was inspired by \\"Texas Flood\\" and \\"Axis: Bold as Love\\".  But, in the meantime, I'll definitely enjoy this album.

B002LVUKIS|5.0|First off I have to say I did not get to writing this review for some time after I picked this game up from Amazon. Ever still I must say I love this series very much and would recommend it to anyone wanting a good stealth series to dive into. I however will not be reviewing as much story as I am game play, and even a added bit for a video fix regarding the first two games. This is intending as being informative while still not being a spoiler, as it is not my intent to tell you about the characters other then listing some factions and the name of the character you play as.<br /><br />So I originally bought this set for my old PC running on XP to find that the original games did not work do to texture loading errors and so on. The Eidos forums supplied no support regarding the first two and their errors (not to my surprise). So I ended up playing Thief: Deadly Shadows cause it was the only one that would run, but I loved it. So after playing through it and wishing there was more I traded it to a friend so that maybe he could get something out of it too.<br /><br />Amazingly after Ordering a Falcon Northwest PC running Windows 7, all the games ran fine after getting the set back from my friend I lent it too. Only thing that had problems were the videos that needed a simple fix for the first two in the series. Here it is before I start in review of the individual games.<br /><br />  _______________________________________________________________________<br /><br />Thief1/2 VIDEO FIX BELOW.<br />If your videos don't play on the first two, here is a fix I use for it that with some intermediate knowledge of computers is simple to do. To test if it works run the intro on the main menu to see if your fix works. Excerpt from a support page on The Looking Glass Forums.<br /><br />\\"Scrooge's Permanent Movie Fix<br />1) Locate (use \\"Search\\") the file named \\"regsvr32.exe\\". It is located in the Windows SYSTEM directory (if 9x/ME) or SYSTEM32 (if 2K/XP). Copy it in your thief installation.<br /><br />2) Create a text document named \\"RegIndeo.bat\\" and copy/paste the following text:<br />\\"regsvr32.exe /s LGVID.AX\\" (without quotes)<br /><br />3) Save it (The file extension must be .bat)<br /><br />Simply double-click on the file when your movies don't play.<br /><br />Note: it is not needed to repeat the operation in all your installed Thief/Gold/II, simply use one of them. The \\"registration\\" process will fix the problems in all installed Thief games present.\\"<br /><br />  _____________________________________________________________________________<br /><br />I love these games now that I have been able to play them, and consider them to be the most unique and enjoyable stealth games out there. Also, out of my lurking nature developed from playing these games, I really couldn't see stealth any other way. I am new to this series as I picked them up early this year, so I am no old fan. I wish I had found this sooner, but now am just thankful I had found them in the first place. So to the reviews you wanted to read...<br /><br />Thief: Gold. For an old game this certainly had some broad levels that were quite dynamic for the time. It gave a lot to story while keeping the game's levels interesting with unexpected twists. One thing that has always been in Thief games that started in the first one is their ability to completely change your objectives and throw you for a loop during a mission. So these games are never that predictable and have great moments that force you to think for an alternative. Many weapons in your armament have unique abilities to overcome obstacles, neutralize them, or even destroy them. Some things have been around ever since the first game and I will list them here...<br /><br />Black Jack: This is a basic weapon that is handy for knocking out opponents from behind with out alerting nearby enemies. It is however near worthless in a fight so is a weapon of stealth only.<br /><br />Gas Arrow: This is another stealth weapon that can knock out multiple opponents within a close radius. This is a more expensive option but allows you to remain in a safe position while silently knocking out enemies.<br /><br />Moss Arrow: In times when the floor you need to cross will make to much noise and there is no way around, Moss Arrows are another key to stealth. These arrows spawn a patch of moss that covers a decent radius of a floor making footsteps in that patch silent.<br /><br />Noise Maker Arrow: When you gotta make a break around a couple of guards, these can be used to distract them. They are very noisy as the name says so point it where you want them to look into.<br /><br />Water Arrow: This douses fire from torches to most fire places. This is one of the most practical weapons that darkens areas so that you remain unseen.<br /><br />Flash Bombs: When times get tough and you don't feel like fighting, throw one of these to blind those guards for a short time. Note that you can blind yourself to if you are not careful with one of these.<br /><br />Broad Head Arrow: A basic arrow that is used to damage or kill enemies.<br /><br />Fire Arrow: A much more damaging arrow that is useful for very tough enemies.<br /><br />Mines: A even greater step to insuring your enemy's ultimate demise would be to throw one of these in front of their path. Note that in the first two games you can also set these off so be careful with them.<br /><br />  _________________________________________________<br /><br />The Thief games on the Dark Engine (being the first and second Thief games) worked off of a set of missions with videos connecting them with story. So that is why the videos are so essential to them and is why I also included the fix in this review. So if you are getting video trouble, again I prompt you to go through the steps of the fix, otherwise you lose the whole story and will not have a very informed or enjoyable experience.<br /><br />In this installment the factions of the Hammerites, and the Pagans are introduced.<br /><br />Thief: The Metal Age. So as a direct continuation to the first game, with some minor improvements along the way I think it held it's own in the series. While some considered it to be far less, I think it included so much and had much more vastness to some of the levels that it really deserved a lot more from fans. To me I honestly liked this one for the level design over the first, as it was far less glitchy as the first. To me this was an equal extension, but it had far enough to keep it fresh with both story and design. This gives a much more industrial side to the series, and accents it quite nicely.<br /><br />In this installment the notable Mechanist faction, a deviant version of the Hammerites, comes on the scene as the main part of the story.<br /><br />Thief: Deadly Shadows. Being that this was released four years after the former game Thief 2 I will try and sum up things that changed and that were kept. Items that were taken out or changed go as listed.<br /><br />  _________________________________________________________<br /><br />Rope and Vine arrows: These were replaced by a more physics practical item known as the wall climbing gloves. However the gloves are not available to the user until later on in the game, which some considered to be a fault in the game.<br /><br />Speed Potion: This was not included in T3. This also includes Slow fall potion, as well as Invisibility potion.<br /><br />Sword: This was replaced by the dagger, that in my opinion was a good change because it was not as heavy and made more sense for a Thief to have. Still has a one hit kill on a unsuspecting opponent, otherwise known as the back stab.<br /><br />Frog Beast: These little guys were not included most likely because Garret the character you play as was not associated with the Pagan faction in story any more.<br /><br />Keys: These are now less cumbersome and once acquired just make doors open and do not need a key to be selected from inventory. This was just a simpler step to be less confusing.<br /><br />Lock Picking: This is now a more realistic sort of mini-scene that uses the mouse positioning as the player's hand with the pick. It goes in steps that unlock in procession upon hitting the sweet spot with the mouse.<br /><br />Player Physics, and Environment Interactions: While not being the most state of the art as what might have been expected by this time, they still are a improvement from the original two games. They still have some glitches such as the player getting stuck on detail objects forcing you to reload the previous saved game. There is still no finesse in setting an object down as just dropping a held physics prop still is very loud and alerts near enemies if present. While that may not seem so bad it can be annoying if you happen to pick up a object by accident. My advice is find a corner away from those you do not want to alert and drop your held object there.<br /><br />  _________________________________________________________________________<br /><br />I may have missed some items but those are the general things that are noticeably different from the Dark Engine installments to the Unreal 2 version as T3. So now to my review of Thief: Deadly Shadows. As I will refer to it as T3, it has some characteristics that do change it's feel over the first two. It retains the same design and story within the existing universe, but expands in the areas of graphics and story. Overall the plot seems much more interwoven and well written then the first games. For also changing studios it did well I think to continue after a four year void of no sequels.<br /><br />Game play features a lot of the classic strategies as the original ones did. There is controversy over the lack in changing or adding a immense new world, but this feature was also a ported to the Xbox so the game was created to fit what the Xbox could handle at that time. It also held a bit more of a fresh start for the new player base on the console, so thus the game could not simply be named Thief 3, and took on it's title it has now. This created some frustration for long time players since Eidos had catered to the console generation a little more for the continuing Thief game they had been waiting for.<br /><br />T3 works differently in the way the levels pan out. It still takes place in the city, but it allows you to explore and travel as you will through loading gates, instead of being guided mission by mission as the old predecessors did. However T3 does have actual missions but it lets you tackle them when you are ready, and in almost every case you have to travel to the mission start point, that is usually denoted with a glowing Keeper glyph. This is one of the top reasons I love T3 so much, is that it is very open world designed and is not linear as so many games are. This can be confusing some times but rewards you with that great feeling when you accomplished your goals on your own. This installment is best described as a much more real feeling Thief game over the previous ones. There is more I could add about game play, but I think this has given enough info regarding what these games are relatively about. I hope this improves your experience and further convinces you into buying this great series.gans are introduced.<br /><br />Thief: The Metal Age.
