B0055PGUYU|3.0|The first thesis of the book is that habits work by a  cycle of trigger, action, reward process.<br /><br />The second thesis is that what makes the cycle so persistent is the energy of craving behind it.<br /><br />Habits control much of our life. They are a very efficient means of processing information -- thousands of time faster than our conscious thinking mind.  So they are very, very useful. But they can set traps for us by causing us to behave in ways that are counter to our better natures.<br /><br />And they can allow others to manipulate us.<br /><br />This book points that out, and has some data to support its theses of the habit cycle and the craving that gives them a life and energy of their own.<br /><br />That data draws on advertising and market research and history. In that aspect, it's more empirical than most explanations of habit.<br /><br />The theses are interesting - but ultimately not convincing as  a complete explanation.<br /><br />So, how do you change habits? The book's premise is that the first step is becoming aware of the triggers. Once the triggers are found, the author recommends forgetting about the why, and working instead to substitute a better action than the current outcome.  That's probably a reasonable starting point for most people.<br /><br />A key element in changing the action associated with a habit is a belief that the action can be changed. That's critical. If you don't think you can change the habit, you won't.<br /><br />Another useful element in changing habits is community. People can help you see both your triggers and actions, - even when they've become mostly invisible to you.<br /><br />There are a few more ideas in the book worth considering. One of them is that there are key habits that spill over into everything else. Change those, and the effects of the changed actions will ripple through your personality.<br /><br />Another idea is about the role of habits in a bureaucracy, such as in a corporate structure.In that environment, habits have an additional role of defining acceptable behavior among competing groups with different interests.<br /><br />The author also explores the moral consequences of habits. Are you morally responsible for your habits, if in fact, they override or ignore your ability to see and choose consciously? Or, are you only responsible when you know you have certain habits and don't change them?<br /><br />On a scale of 5, I'd give this book a 3 .<br /><br />It is somewhat thought provoking for those who will use it as starting point for looking at the role of habits in their own life.  But it is ultimately unconvincing and a bit shallow for those who really want to grapple with deeply ingrained habits that limit their lives.<br /><br />And I suppose some marketing types will find some ideas here about how to use people's habits to manipulate and exploit them.

0156034026|3.0|The concept of this book is rather unique and interesting. Several books about post-9/11 America have been published, but few, if any, tackle the rising \\"Islamaphobia\\" that popped up afterwards. This story, which is told from the point-of-view of a Pakistani immigrant living and working at a prestigious firm in New York City, does a good job of showing that side of the story. Now, Changez may or may not be the most likeable character and a few readers may take offense to some of his actions and reactions in the book (e.g., as the review above notes, Changez \\"smiled\\" when he saw the Twin Towers get hit), but I think that makes him a more complex and interesting character. His relationships with other characters, especially Erica (who you may like or plain hate) is what makes up the bulk of this book beyond post-9/11 issues.<br /><br />The flaws of this book distracts a bit from the story. First off, it's obvious within the first sentence that this story is told from a second person point-of-view. It's a tricky way of telling a story, especially a novel of this length, and honestly, I don't think it really worked. You get glimpses and hints about the person, the American, he's talking to, but not enough to really make him a well realized character. The way this story is told becomes more annoying when Changez isn't telling his life story, when the story jumps back to present day Pakistan and pulled me out of the story more than once. It's hard to explain, but some of the sentences, such as \\"Oh, I can see that you are looking at me quizzically\\" (note, this sentence doesn't really appear in the story, but lines similar to this do crop up) just come off as odd and mess up the flow. The ending of the story will either satisfy you or drive you bonkers-personally, I wasn't a fan of the ending.<br /><br />It's definitely worth a read, especially since it has been adapted into a movie that should be coming out in the next few months/2013. If you don't let the  manner in whichthis story is told trip you up, you'll find some very good story telling in here.

B003MC5N28|4.0|I first read this book back in middle school. So, I decided to revisit this classic tale now that I have more appreciation for literature than my pre-teen self.<br /><br />At first the writing seemed overly simple, lacking in any deep descriptive detail, but as I read, I found how well it seemed to fit with the tone and the story overall. The tale is told from the perspective of a young boy in a highly controlled society where even emotions are kept under tight wraps. All that changes when he is chosen to be the next Receiver, keeper of the society's memories. Things start to unfold gradually, then a little more rapidly as time goes on. The boy learns that the world used to be different, and how people gave up many things, like pain and emotions and feelings of attachment, in order not to feel that way anymore, if everything is the same, then no one would have to suffer anymore. A sort of play on the famous saying, 'It's better to have love and lost...', except this society truly believes it's better to have never loved at all.<br /><br />I believe the themes in this book are pretty poignant, even in this day. How much are we willing to give up in order to feel safe and protected? Our rights? Our families? Even our humanity? Jonas slowly starts to realize this as the tale progresses and he learns dark secrets about the family and home that he grew up with, delightfully ignorant to all that is around him. I loved his curiosity to find out more, to experience even the memories that were painful.<br /><br />Overall, this book is not an exciting read. There aren't many action scenes. But it is an enjoyable one, one I was glad I revisited now that I am older, and I would like to believe, somewhat wiser. However, I was a little disappointed by the ending. There was so much build up, so many questions that begged to be answered, but sadly they weren't. Where did Jonas go? Is the rest of the world like the society he lived in? There were so many. Instead, we are left with aslight hope and a town that somehow reminded me of the Christmas town from Nightmare Before Christmas...
